href=\"#apjaad565bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib14\">2017</a>).\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/suppdata/apjaad565t5_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aad565\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/suppdata/apjaad565t5_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div><h3 id=\"apjaad565s5-3\" name=\"apjaad565s5-3\">5.3.\u00a0Emission-line Measurement of the Star Formation Rate</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We measure equivalent widths from the spectral extractions centered on the core of the galaxy (shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f5\">5</a>). The region around [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] and H<em>\u03b1</em> are modeled with the sum of the best-FIT <tt>FAST</tt> SED model (convolved to the <tt>ppxf</tt> velocity dispersion of 305 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) and Gaussian emission. We fix the emission component to have a velocity dispersion of 305 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>. For [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], we use two Gaussians, with the relative amplitude fixed to 0.74 for the 3726 \u00c5 component and 0.26 for the 3729 \u00c5 one.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Host-galaxy photometry and spectrum with the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> model. The <em>HST</em> photometry was used for flux calibration of the X-shooter spectrum. The best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> model for both BC03 and FSPS templates is a massive galaxy with low SFR <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn26.gif\" alt=\"$0.4\\pm 0.2$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.4\\pm 0.2</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>. The age, SFR timescale parameter <em>\u03c4</em>, and rest-frame <em>U</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em> and <em>V</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>J</em> colors (well-constrained by the GTC, <em>HST</em>, and <em>Spitzer</em> photometry) all imply a quiescent galaxy; with an age that is 10 times the e-folding timescale of the best-fit stellar population model, 99.94% of stars expected to ever form in this galaxy have already formed.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0In blue, we show cutouts of the spectral regions for [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (left panel) and H<em>\u03b1</em> (right panel). No emission lines are detected in either region. In black, we show the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> template, convolved to the <tt>ppxf</tt> velocity dispersion of 305 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>. In red, we show the best-fit [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (left panel) and H<em>\u03b1</em> (right panel) contribution.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We find a [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>]\u00a0<em>\u03bb</em>3727 \u00c5 rest-frame equivalent width of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn27.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{EW}([{\\rm{O}}\\,{\\rm{II}}])={1.5}_{-2.8}^{+3.0}\\,\\mathring{\\rm A} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{EW}([{\\rm{O}}\\,{\\rm{II}}])={1.5}_{-2.8}^{+3.0}\\,\\mathring{\\rm A}</script></span></span>, as shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f5\">5</a>. We see no evidence of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]\u00a0<em>\u03bb</em>5007 \u00c5 emission; a nearby strong night sky line residual prevents us from measuring this spectral region. After correcting for the stellar Balmer absorption predicted by the <tt>FAST</tt> SED fit, we measure H<em>\u03b1</em> having EW(H<em>\u03b1</em>)\u00a0=\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn28.gif\" alt=\"${1.1}_{-1.7}^{+1.9}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{1.1}_{-1.7}^{+1.9}</script></span></span> \u00c5. These weak emission line equivalent widths point to a low SFR per unit stellar mass.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn11\">29</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>Using the X-Shooter calibration discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s5-2\">5.2</a>, and correcting for extinction, we convert our H<em>\u03b1</em> equivalent width to a line luminosity of <em>L</em>(H<em>\u03b1</em>)\u00a0=\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn29.gif\" alt=\"${2.4}_{-3.7}^{+4.1}\\times {10}^{41}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{2.4}_{-3.7}^{+4.1}\\times {10}^{41}</script></span></span> erg s<sup>\u22121</sup>.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn12\">30</a></sup>\n We then convert to a star formation rate using <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn32.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{M}=5.45\\times {10}^{-42}\\,L({\\rm{H}}\\alpha )$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{M}=5.45\\times {10}^{-42}\\,L({\\rm{H}}\\alpha )</script></span></span> (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>)/(erg s<sup>\u22121</sup>) from Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib13\">2010</a>), resulting in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn33.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{M}={1.3}_{-2.0}^{+2.3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{M}={1.3}_{-2.0}^{+2.3}</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>. The H<em>\u03b1</em> and SED-fit star formation rates agree well, within <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn34.gif\" alt=\"$0.4$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.4</script></span></span> <em>\u03c3</em>. At low SFR, O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> emission can be badly contaminated by emission from post-AGB stars (e.g., Belfiore et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib5\">2016</a>). If there were no such contamination, the derived SFR would be <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn35.gif\" alt=\"${9}_{-17}^{+20}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{9}_{-17}^{+20}</script></span></span> (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>)/(erg s<sup>\u22121</sup>), using the conversion <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn36.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{M}=2.65\\times {10}^{-41}\\,L({\\rm{O}}\\,{\\rm{II}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{M}=2.65\\times {10}^{-41}\\,L({\\rm{O}}\\,{\\rm{II}})</script></span></span> (Meyers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib59\">2012</a>) and our <tt>FAST</tt> SED extinction-corrected [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>]\u00a0<em>\u03bb</em>3727 \u00c5 luminosity of <em>L</em>([O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>]) <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn37.gif\" alt=\"${3.3}_{-6.2}^{+7.6}\\times {10}^{41}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{3.3}_{-6.2}^{+7.6}\\times {10}^{41}</script></span></span> (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>)/(erg s<sup>\u22121</sup>). This SFR estimate is consistent with the others (although with much larger uncertainties). However, we do not use it to estimate the SFR for this host. Likewise, while [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]\u00a0<em>\u03bb</em>5007 \u00c5 is usually present when there is star formation, it is a poor SFR indicator due to its strong dependence on metallicity and the hardness of the stellar UV spectrum.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaad565s5-4\" name=\"apjaad565s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0Profile Fitting</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We also measure the surface brightness profile of the host galaxy. We fit the <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> imaging, as it is close to rest-frame <em>B</em>-band. To determine the central profile given the undersampled imaging, we again use a forward-model approach. We model the observed pixels (without resampling) of nine dithers (from SN-free epochs) with a S\u00e9rsic profile (S\u00e9rsic <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib89\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib89\">1963</a>) convolved with the PSF (the same PSF as for the SN photometry). We marginalize over the amplitude, half-light radius, ellipticity, and orientation.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn13\">31</a></sup>\n The galaxy is fit well by this simple model, with no evidence of an on-core point source (or variability), which might imply a central AGN (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f6\">6</a>). We find a S\u00e9rsic index of 2.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1, and a de-magnified half-light radius of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn38.gif\" alt=\"$0.679\\pm 0.017$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.679\\pm 0.017</script></span></span>\u00a0kpc. In combination with the total stellar mass, this implies central density of log<sub>10</sub>\u03a3(<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn39.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{kpc}}^{-2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{\\odot }\\,{\\mathrm{kpc}}^{-2}</script></span></span>)\u00a0=\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn40.gif\" alt=\"$10.62\\pm 0.02$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">10.62\\pm 0.02</script></span></span>, as summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t5\">5</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Radial brightness profile of the host galaxy (black points), with the best-fit 2D S\u00e9rsic model (red points). The scatter in the best-fit S\u00e9rsic model is a combination of the elliptical galaxy plotted in radial coordinates, and the asymmetric WFC3 IR PSF. The galaxy is well-fit by the 2D S\u00e9rsic profile, with a best-fit S\u00e9rsic index of 2.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 and a half-light radius of <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn41.gif\" alt=\"$0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 225\\pm 0.006$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 225\\pm 0.006</script></span></span>. The galactocentric radius at which the SN occurred is indicated by a vertical blue line at 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjaad565s5-5\" name=\"apjaad565s5-5\">5.5.\u00a0Host-galaxy Summary</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>All host parameters point to the host galaxy being a massive quiescent galaxy. The velocity dispersion from the spectrum and the stellar mass from SED fitting provide joint confirmation of a high mass, while the SED fit to the spectrum and photometry provides a strong constraint on low SFR, reinforced by lack of evidence for emission lines in the spectrum. The derived specific SFR of log<sub>10</sub>(sSFR)\u00a0=\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn42.gif\" alt=\"$-{11.48}_{-0.30}^{+0.17}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">-{11.48}_{-0.30}^{+0.17}</script></span></span> qualifies this galaxy as being in a stage of quiescent star formation, as defined by the mass-doubling criterion of Feulner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib26\">2005</a>) or the log<sub>10</sub>(sSFR)\u00a0&lt; \u221211 criterion of Dom\u00ednguez S\u00e1nchez et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib24\">2011</a>). The 10 star formation <em>e</em>-folding times that have passed reinforce this conclusion. The structure of the host galaxy\u2014its moderately high S\u00e9rsic index, small <em>r</em><sub>eff</sub>, and high central density\u2014is also consistent with other massive quiescent galaxies found at similar redshifts (Muzzin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib62\">2012</a>; van der Wel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib102\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib102\">2014</a>; Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib64\">2015</a>; van Dokkum et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib103\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib103\">2015</a>; Barro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib4\">2017</a>).</p><p>At these high redshifts, it has become standard practice to define quiescence photometrically, based on the statistical separation of galaxies in rest-frame <em>U</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em> versus <em>V</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>J</em> (the <em>UVJ</em> diagram; Williams et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib106\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib106\">2009</a>; Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib10\">2011</a>) into a red, dead \"clump\" and a star-forming \"track\" derived using the UV+IR SFR. Spectroscopic follow-up has confirmed that the <em>UVJ</em> diagram selects older, quiescent galaxies efficiently (Whitaker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib105\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib105\">2013</a>). In this space, dusty star-forming galaxies move along a track that is perpendicular to the separation between the quiescent clump and the star-forming track (Williams et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib106\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib106\">2009</a>; Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib10\">2011</a>). Movement from the star-forming track to the quiescent clump is well-described by passive stellar evolution (Williams et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib106\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib106\">2009</a>). The unpopulated region between the quiescent clump and the star-forming track is known as the the \"green valley\"; it is not yet established whether these galaxies represent a short-lived (and dusty) evolutionary path from the star formation main sequence to the red sequence of galaxies, or are merely the overlapping tails of two distinct populations with measurement scatter (see, e.g., Pandya et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib67\">2017</a>, for a review). Regardless, the <em>U</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em> and <em>V</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>J</em> colors of the galaxy place it firmly in the quiescent regime according to the <em>UVJ</em> classification scheme (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t5\">5</a> and, e.g., Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib10\">2011</a>).</p><p>The lack of emission lines that trace star formation, the low specific SFR and stellar extinction, the red color of the SED from rest-frame <em>U</em> to <em>J</em>, and estimates of morphology from the imaging combine to strongly imply a quiescent galaxy, and not a dusty star-forming galaxy.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn14\">32</a></sup>\n Of course very sharp-edged dust structures, such that the affected stars contribute negligibly to the light and associated extinction we do detect, could hide more stellar mass and star formation. Such dust would also be required to preserve the smooth structure of this galaxy. In this particular case, such a contrived means of hiding significant stellar mass is especially unlikely since this galaxy is already on the steeply falling segment of the galaxy luminosity function. For instance, if half the stellar mass were hidden, the 2.0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a02.5 galaxy luminosity function (e.g., Davidzon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib23\">2017</a>) predicts a 65<b>&#x00d7;</b> lower incidence per unit volume for the resulting galaxy. We also cannot rule out a situation in which the specific line of sight for this SN has much less dust, thereby escaping these constraints from host galaxy properties. But such a finely tuned case seems unlikely, and is unnecessary to explain our observations, as the following section will make apparent.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s6\" name=\"apjaad565s6\">6.\u00a0SN Photometric Classification</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>It was not possible to obtain a spectrum of the SN itself, so we use our photometry, host-galaxy redshift, and host-galaxy star formation rate to ascertain the SN type. The major types we wish to distinguish are SNe\u00a0Ia and CC SNe. Within each of these are subtypes: SNe\u00a0II and SNe\u00a0Ibc among the CC SNe, and normal and abnormal subtypes among the SNe\u00a0Ia. In order to arrive at an estimate for the probability of each SN type to be the type for SN\u00a0SCP16C03, we must take into account several factors. The first is the average relative likelihood for each (sub)type, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn43.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{type}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{type}}</script></span></span>, derived using the light curve fits. Then, we need an estimate of the a priori SN\u00a0Ia fraction, <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub>, for the specific host of SN\u00a0SCP16C03. An estimate of the a priori relative incidence of the normal versus abnormal subtypes for SNe\u00a0Ia and the SN\u00a0II and SN\u00a0Ibc subtypes for CC SNe that correspond to our light curve templates is also needed. The product of <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> with these subtype apportionments gives the a priori expectation, <em>f</em><sub>type</sub>, for each type of template. These can then be combined to give the probability of each (sub)type based on our measurements:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaad565eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565eqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{P}_{\\mathrm{type}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{{f}_{\\mathrm{type}}\\,{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{type}}/{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}}{\\sum {f}_{\\mathrm{type}}\\,{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{type}}/{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><h3 id=\"apjaad565s6-1\" name=\"apjaad565s6-1\">6.1.\u00a0Light-curve Likelihoods</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Quantitative photometric typing is made more challenging by the limited signal-to-noise, wavelength coverage, and phase coverage of our light curves. Similar to the photometric typing efforts performed for other high-redshift SNe (Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib76\">2012</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib75\">2015a</a>; Jones et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib39\">2013</a>; Nordin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>; Patel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib68\">2014</a>), as well as the Photometric Supernova Identifier (Sako et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib82\">2011</a>), we fit the photometry with a range of templates to compare light curve shape and color. Non-parametric methods have shown comparable promise (e.g., Kessler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib44\">2010</a>; Lochner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib52\">2016</a>), but better temporal sampling than available here would be necessary to implement these.</p><p>The core-collapse templates are taken from SNANA (Kessler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib45\">2009</a>), some of which are described in Gilliland et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib29\">1999</a>), Nugent et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib66\">2002</a>), Stern et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib95\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib95\">2004</a>), Levan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib50\">2005</a>), and Sako et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib82\">2011</a>), and are implemented in <tt>sncosmo</tt>.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn15\">33</a></sup>\n When multiple versions of the same non-Ia template are available, we conservatively take that with the lowest <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> (giving that non-Ia SN template the highest probability of matching). We do not expect the CC template set to be complete for several reasons. Typically, the absolute magnitudes of CC SNe are fainter than those of SNe\u00a0Ia (Richardson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib73\">2014</a>), so proportionately fewer are found. Furthermore, the CC SNe that are discovered are less likely to be followed up (because they are not as cosmologically useful).</p><p>For the SN\u00a0Ia templates, we construct a random sample with the latest version (SALT2-4) of the Spectral Adaptive Lightcurve Template model (Guy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib34\">2007</a>; Mosher et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib61\">2014</a>; SALT2 is described more in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s7\">7</a>), with Gaussian distributions of the light-curve shape parameters <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>c</em>, centered at zero and having dispersions of 1 in <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> and 0.1 in <em>c</em>. Because SALT2 is a parameterized model, we use it to simulate a distribution of SNe\u00a0Ia. We also include the Hsiao et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib37\">2007</a>) SN\u00a0Ia spectral template, the Nugent et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib66\">2002</a>) templates for normal and underluminous SN\u00a01991bg-like SN\u00a0Ia subtypes, and the Stern et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib95\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib95\">2004</a>) template for the overluminous SN\u00a01991T-like SN\u00a0Ia subtype. SALT2 does not mimic the SN\u00a01991bg-like and SN\u00a01991T-like subtypes well, so we separately keep track of those subtypes. The templates of normal SNe\u00a0Ia from Hsiao et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib37\">2007</a>) and Nugent et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib66\">2002</a>) are only used as a cross-check of our SALT2 results.</p><p>Next we fit the templates to the data. We leave the date of maximum and normalization (i.e., luminosity) fully unconstrained. As the unreddened colors of the templates are unknown, it is necessary to allow the relative extinction between SN\u00a0SCP16C03 and the SN used to determine the template colors to vary between positive and negative values. We achieve this using a broad, double-sided exponential prior having a scale length of 0.2 in <em>E</em>(<em>B</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em>). As <em>R</em><sub><em>V</em></sub> cannot be constrained by the data, we fix it to the Galactic value of 3.1 (e.g., Schlafly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib86\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib86\">2016</a>) for all SN types. It is also necessary to account for the fact that the parameter space is sampled with only a finite number of templates. As with previous work (Rodney &amp; Tonry <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib79\">2009</a>), we add 0.15 mag uncertainty in quadrature with each photometry point to address this.</p><p>The three best-fitting templates (with a spread in log-likelihood of just 0.2) are the SALT2 SN\u00a0Ia template (close to the mean of the <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>c</em> distributions), the Nugent template for SN\u00a01991T-like SNe\u00a0Ia, and an SDSS SN\u00a0II (SN 2007md). Encouragingly, the best-fit SALT2 template has a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> of 8.7 for 8 DoF (including the SALT2 model uncertainties, but not the 0.15 mag uncertainty floor). Other templates with a best-fit likelihood within a factor of 20 of the SN\u00a0Ia template are the Nugent SN\u00a0Ia template, an SDSS SN\u00a0Ib/c template (SN 2006fo), and the Hsiao SN\u00a0Ia template.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn16\">34</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>From the light curve fits, we obtain relative likelihoods of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn44.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}91{\\rm{T}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}91{\\rm{T}}}</script></span></span>/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn45.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>\u00a0=\u00a02.67, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn46.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}91\\mathrm{bg}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}91\\mathrm{bg}}</script></span></span>/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn47.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>\u00a0=\u00a00, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn48.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ibc}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ibc}}</script></span></span>/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn49.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>\u00a0=\u00a00.05, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn50.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{II}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{II}}</script></span></span>/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn51.gif\" alt=\"${\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\overline{L}}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>\u00a0=\u00a00.07. The highest likelihood (best-fit) points in normal SN\u00a0Ia parameter space are slightly higher than for the SN 1991T-like template, but the average likelihood for the normal SNe\u00a0Ia population is lower due to points with extreme <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> or <em>c</em> values (i.e., those that do not match the data well). Because the likelihood for the SN\u00a01991bg-like template is so remote, we do not consider this subtype further.</p><p>It is worth noting that the two CC SNe are <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />2 mag fainter in absolute magnitude than any reasonable value for SN\u00a0SCP16C03 (we discuss the lensing amplification in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s8\">8</a>), so our typing analysis is very conservative since absolute magnitude is not included in the evaluation (see Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565app1\">A</a>). However, we do not have enough templates derived from CC SNe in the same absolute magnitude range as SNe\u00a0Ia, so we include these fainter CC SNe for now.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaad565s6-2\" name=\"apjaad565s6-2\">6.2.\u00a0The Intrinsic Fraction of SNe\u00a0Ia</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The next ingredient for computing an overall probability of being a SN\u00a0Ia is an estimate of the relative rates of CC SNe and SNe\u00a0Ia in the host galaxy. We follow Meyers et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib59\">2012</a>) and estimate the <em>intrinsic</em> SN\u00a0Ia fraction (i.e., without imposing any detection limit). Starting from relations for the relative rates of SNe\u00a0Ia<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn17\">35</a></sup>\n and CC SNe,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn18\">36</a></sup>\n based on SFR and stellar mass, along with our measurements of these properties\u2014log<sub>10</sub>(<em>M</em>/<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>)\u00a0= <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn52.gif\" alt=\"$11.08\\pm 0.02$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">11.08\\pm 0.02</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn53.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{M}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{M}</script></span></span> = <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn54.gif\" alt=\"$0.4\\pm 0.2$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.4\\pm 0.2</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>from Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t5\">5</a> of Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s5\">5</a>\u2014our estimated fraction of SNe\u00a0Ia is</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaad565eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565eqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}\\begin{array}{rcl}{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}} & = & {\\left[1+\\displaystyle \\frac{{R}_{\\mathrm{CC}}}{{R}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}}\\right]}^{-1}\\\\ & = & \\ {\\left[1+\\displaystyle \\frac{6.8\\times {10}^{-3}\\dot{M}}{5.3\\times {10}^{-14}M+3.9\\times {10}^{-4}\\dot{M}}\\right]}^{-1}\\\\ & = & \\ {\\left[1+\\displaystyle \\frac{0.0028{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}}{0.0065{\\mathrm{yr}}^{-1}}\\right]}^{-1}=0.70.\\end{array}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>Thus, we expect that 70% of the SNe exploding in this host galaxy at the epoch when SN\u00a0SCP16C03 was observed will be SNe\u00a0Ia. We note that <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn55.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span> as calculated here is essentially independent of the assumed amplification, as both the SFR and stellar mass have the same scaling with amplification, and thus it cancels out of Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>). Moreover, these rates integrate over all SNe, regardless of their luminosity, and amplification cannot change the intrinsic ratio. We further note that <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn56.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span> does not depend on the spatial distribution of star formation in the host galaxy. For example, if there is <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn57.gif\" alt=\"$0.4\\pm 0.2$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.4\\pm 0.2</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup> of star formation occurring in a satellite galaxy, then 70% of the SNe in the combined system will still be SNe\u00a0Ia. Because all SNe are included, and because most CC SNe are fainter than most SNe\u00a0Ia, this calculation of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn58.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span> will be lower than the actual observed SN\u00a0Ia fraction.</p><p>In Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565app1\">A</a> we perform an analysis in which typical SN luminosity distributions, and the detection efficiency of our survey, are included, in addition to allowing for an extra starburst component added to the old stellar population from the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> model. We address whether enhanced star formation obscured by dust at the SN location, in combination with amplification by MOO\u00a0J1014+0038, could enhance the observed fraction of CC\u00a0SNe, concluding that for realistic amounts of amplification, it does not.</p><p>Since photometric typing is the primary means by which large upcoming SN cosmology surveys plan to obtain large numbers of SNe\u00a0Ia, it is worth noting that <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> could go as low as 0.05 for an extreme starburst in a low-mass host galaxy. Thus, this prior can significantly alter photometric classification probabilities across the full range of SN\u00a0Ia host galaxies.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaad565s6-3\" name=\"apjaad565s6-3\">6.3.\u00a0The Intrinsic Fraction of Subtypes within Each Major Type</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The final ingredient required for photometric classification of SN\u00a0SCP16C03 is the relative incidence in nature of the subtypes\u2014normal SN\u00a0Ia and SN\u00a01991T-like subtypes among SNe\u00a0Ia, and SN\u00a0II and SN\u00a0Ibc among CC SNe\u2014corresponding to the light curve template categories that we use. We take a ratio of 3-1 for SNe\u00a0II to SNe\u00a0Ib/c (Li et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib51\">2011</a>). We therefore apportion the CC fraction (1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn59.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>) into 0.75 (1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn60.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>) for SNe\u00a0II and 0.25 (1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn61.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span>) for SNe\u00a0Ibc. In volume-limited nearby samples, 3% of SNe\u00a0Ia are pure SN\u00a01991T-like (Scalzo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib84\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib84\">2012</a>; Silverman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib90\">2012</a>). Another 5% of nearby SNe are SN\u00a01999aa-like (Silverman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib90\">2012</a>), while at high redshift we derive a SN\u00a01999aa-like fraction of 1%\u20133% from Balland et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib3\">2009</a>). However, SN\u00a01999aa-like SNe are only marginally overluminous, and so these can be treated with the other normal SNe. We therefore apportion <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn62.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span> into 0.03 <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn63.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span> for SN\u00a01991T-like and 0.97 <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn64.gif\" alt=\"${f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{f}_{\\mathrm{Ia}}</script></span></span> for normal SNe\u00a0Ia (i.e., also excluding SN 1991bg-like or SN 2002cx-like SNe Ia). Although these subtype fractions are derived at low redshift and may be different at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22, the assumed values do not strongly affect the classification as a SN\u00a0Ia. Thus, our estimated fractions of each type of SN, <em>f</em><sub>type</sub>, are <em>f</em><sub>IaNormal</sub>\u00a0=\u00a068% for normal SN\u00a0Ia, <em>f</em><sub>Ia91T</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02% SN\u00a01991T-like SN\u00a0Ia, <em>f</em><sub>II</sub>\u00a0=\u00a022% SN\u00a0II, and <em>f</em><sub>Ib/c</sub>\u00a0=\u00a07% SN\u00a0Ib/c.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn19\">37</a></sup>\n\n</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaad565s6-4\" name=\"apjaad565s6-4\">6.4.\u00a0SN Classification Results</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Combining the likelihoods from the light-curve fitting, with the product of the fraction of SN types in nature and the SN\u00a0Ia fraction from the host-galaxy properties, we obtain our estimates of the overall probability of each type. Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn1\">1</a>) gives a 90.1% chance that SN\u00a0SCP16C03 is a normal SN\u00a0Ia, a 7.4% chance it is a SN\u00a01991T-like SN\u00a0Ia, a 0.5% chance it is a SN\u00a0Ib/c, and a 2.0% chance of it is a SN\u00a0II. This analysis is summarized in the top left panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f7\">7</a>, which shows the highest (best-fit) relative likelihood for each template. The total probability that SN\u00a0SCP16C03 is a SN\u00a0Ia is <em>P</em>(Ia)\u00a0+\u00a0<em>P</em>(Ia 91T)\u00a0=\u00a097.5%. As a final (unlikely) possibility, in Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565app2\">B</a> we evaluate the probability that the SN is merely projected onto the nominal host galaxy, rather than being hosted by it, and find it to be negligible.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0Top left panel: histogram of relative likelihoods from the light curve fits to the templates of each type of SN. The likelihoods are scaled so that the template with the highest likelihood has a value of 1. The histogram is normalized such that the area for each type of SN matches the relative rates expected in this galaxy (Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a>). This histogram indicates that although there are two CC templates with a reasonable fit to the light curves, there are many more that are poor fits and thus the probability that SN\u00a0SCP16C03 is a SN\u00a0Ia is high. Light-curve panels: the best-fit for the SN\u00a0Ia templates (top right), the SN\u00a0II templates (middle left), and the SN\u00a0Ib/c templates (middle right). In the bottom panels, we show how SN\u00a0SCP16C03 compares to the template library in both color (bottom left: the <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>F</em>125<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>125<em>W</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>F</em>160<em>W</em> colors at the epoch with all three) and decline rate (bottom right: the decline between the last two epochs in <em>F</em>105<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>140<em>W</em>). Each point plotted is from the best-fit (i.e., best-fit amplitude, date of maximum, and extinction) of one of the SN templates (for display purposes, we show as many SNe Ia realizations as CC templates, although most SNe in the host galaxy are SNe Ia). The black contours show the 68% confidence intervals from our measurements of SN\u00a0SCP16C03. The blue squares represent SNe\u00a0Ia templates inside both contours; the cyan squares represent SNe\u00a0Ia outside of at least one contour. The consistency of SN\u00a0SCP16C03 with the SN\u00a0Ia distribution is evident. There are no CC SNe in both contours. The red dots represent CC SNe matching the colors, the orange stars represent CC SNe matching the observed declines, and the brown triangles represent CC SNe matching neither colors nor declines.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s7\" name=\"apjaad565s7\">7.\u00a0Light-curve Parameters and Amplification Measurement</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>To compute an amplification estimate, and locate SN\u00a0SCP16C03 in the lower-redshift light-curve parameter distributions, we fit the photometry in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t2\">2</a> with the SALT2 light-curve fitter. SALT2 fits a rest-frame model to the observer-frame photometry, extracting a date of maximum, amplitude (scaled by the inverse squared luminosity distance and typically quoted using a rest-frame <em>B</em>-band magnitude, <em>m</em><sub><em>B</em></sub>), light-curve shape (<em>x</em><sub>1</sub>), and color (<em>c</em>). We find <em>t</em><sub>max</sub>\u00a0=\u00a057474.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.4 MJD, <em>m</em><sub><em>B</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a026.067\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.041 mag, <em>x</em><sub>1</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.54\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.60, and <em>c</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.039\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.051. Our light-curve fit parameters are consistent with the center of the low-redshift <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>c</em> distributions, disfavoring strong evolution in the population mean of these parameters.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn20\">38</a></sup>\n This test would have been difficult to perform with unlensed SNe in this redshift range due to selection effects (the 50% completeness limit at this redshift for unlensed SNe is about \u221219.1, similar to the median absolute magnitude of SNe Ia; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib77\">2015b</a>; B. Hayden et al. 2018, in preparation) and the difficulty of photometrically typing SNe ~1 mag fainter than the amplified SN\u00a0SCP16C03.</p><p>In many previous analyses, linear shape and color standardization has been employed (Tripp <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib101\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib101\">1998</a>)\u2014that is,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaad565eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565eqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\mu }_{B}={m}_{B}+\\alpha \\ {x}_{1}-\\beta \\ c-{M}_{B},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>\u03b1</em> and <em>\u03b2</em> are the slope of the shape and color standardization relations, and <em>M</em><sub><em>B</em></sub> is the absolute <em>B</em>-band magnitude. Here we assume <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, but it drops out of the final analysis of the amplification.</p><p>We take our values of the standardization coefficients, <em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.141, <em>\u03b2</em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u22123.101, from Betoule et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib7\">2014</a>). Evidence is steadily increasing that the <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>c</em> relations are not linear; at a minimum, broken-linear relations are more accurate (Amanullah et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib2\">2010</a>; Suzuki et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib99\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib99\">2012</a>; Scolnic et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib88\">2014</a>; Rubin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib80\">2015</a>; Mandel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib55\">2017</a>; Scolnic &amp; Kessler <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib87\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib87\">2016</a>). For simplicity, we use linear relations here, as this SN is close to the center of both the <em>x</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>c</em> distributions, so applying nonlinear relations makes little difference. The observed absolute magnitude has a weak dependence on host-galaxy stellar mass (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib41\">2010</a>; Sullivan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib97\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib97\">2010</a>), but this relation may be partially driven by progenitor age, and thus would weaken with redshift, as young progenitors will dominate galaxies of both high and low stellar mass (Rigault et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib74\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib74\">2013</a>; Childress et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib18\">2014</a>). However, the lack of detectable emission lines in the host of SN\u00a0SCP16C03 decreases the likelihood that it arose from a young progenitor system. Although most of the likelihood for our galaxy is in the high-mass category (&gt;10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>), we split the difference in <em>M</em><sub><em>B</em></sub>, taking the average of the two host-mass categories (for a value of \u221219.085) and allocating half of the difference as uncertainty (0.035 mag). We assume 0.12 mag of \"intrinsic\" dispersion in the absolute magnitude of high-redshift SNe\u00a0Ia (D. Rubin et al. 2018, in preparation). We also consider the various WFC3 IR systematic uncertainties highlighted in Nordin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>): count-rate nonlinearity, flux-dependent PSF, SED-dependent PSF, and the uncertainty in the CALSPEC system (Bohlin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib8\">2007</a>; Bohlin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib9\">2014</a>). We evaluate the impact of each systematic uncertainty on the distance modulus by varying each one and refitting the SN distance. We then include these differences in the quadrature sum for the total distance uncertainty.</p><p>In order to complete the amplification measurement, we need an estimate of the distance modulus (in the absence of lensing). One method for obtaining this is to compare against a sample of unlensed SNe at similar redshifts (Patel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib68\">2014</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib75\">2015a</a>), but no such sample exists for SN\u00a0SCP16C03. We thus compute the estimated amplification by comparing the SN distance modulus estimate with a cosmological model. We take \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3089 from Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>; including all cosmological data sets), and for simplicity, we assume a flat \u039bCDM cosmology, but we use a large \u00b10.03 Gaussian uncertainty on \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub>. This yields a predicted distance modulus of <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a046.219\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.057, again for <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> (as noted above, <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> drops out of the analysis). As the uncertainty on our measured distance modulus is much larger than the uncertainty on this prediction, the impact of our assumptions is minor. We obtain a distance modulus estimate of 45.11\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.22 mag, and an amplification estimate of 1.10\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.23 mag, with almost all of the measurement uncertainty (0.22 mag) statistical, and not from the cosmological model or systematic uncertainties.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s8\" name=\"apjaad565s8\">8.\u00a0Amplification Interpretation</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>As noted in Nordin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>), we can either test a lensing-derived model with the measured supernova amplification, or improve the lensing-derived model by including our amplification measurement. For the moment, we choose the former, comparing against the weak-lensing (WL) measurements of S. Kim et al. (2018, in preparation), which are derived independently from this analysis. That analysis is based on galaxy shear measurements from the <em>F</em>105<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> imaging data; the total exposure time of each filter exceeded 16,000 s at the time of the analysis. The PSFs are modeled separately using globular cluster observations originally planned for the WFC3 on-orbit calibration. We measure a galaxy shear by fitting a PSF-convolved elliptical Gaussian profile to a galaxy image. After verifying the consistency of the results from both filters, we optimally combine the two shear measurements and create a single source catalog. The redshift distribution of the source population is estimated by utilizing the publicly available UVUDF photometric redshift catalog (Rafelski et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib72\">2015</a>). Because of the limited angular scale, we assume a mass\u2013concentration relation of Duffy et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib25\">2008</a>) in our mass estimation of the cluster while fitting an NFW profile to tangential shears.</p><p>Given that the contours of the WL shear map appear to be reasonably smooth, we assume spherical symmetry for the cluster. We use Monte Carlo methods to derive the predicted amplification at the location of the SN image, assuming Gaussian constraints on the centroid of the cluster and the virial mass, and find <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn66.gif\" alt=\"${0.61}_{-0.16}^{+0.20}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{0.61}_{-0.16}^{+0.20}</script></span></span> mag. This value is compatible at 1.6 <em>\u03c3</em> with the SN amplification measurement (assuming that the uncertainty on the difference is the quadrature sum of the amplification uncertainties). We note that a concentration of bright cluster galaxies lies slightly to the west of the lens center determined from weak-lensing analysis. Their proximity to the SN may boost its amplification. We show contours of the full map (from the median of the Monte Carlo samples) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f1\">1</a>. We have also used the amplification determined from the SN observations to try to estimate the concentration parameter, <em>c</em><sub>200</sub>. While at the current level of observation it is not possible to place strong constraints on the concentration, the inferred value for <em>c</em><sub>200</sub> is consistent with <em>N</em>-body simulations based on \u039bCDM at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.23.</p><p>In previous work with high-redshift SNe, (e.g., Suzuki et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib99\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib99\">2012</a>; Rubin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib81\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib81\">2013</a>; Nordin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>), we were able to make use of \"blinded\" analyses, in which the results are hidden until the analysis is finalized (e.g., Conley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib21\">2006</a>; Maccoun &amp; Perlmutter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib53\">2015</a>). In those works, we were able to blind both the final photometry and the final typing, as both improved over the course of the analysis. In our analysis of SN\u00a0SCP16C03, we approached the photometry and typing with a mature pipeline, and made the decision to trigger follow-up based on this pipeline. Those results cannot be taken to be blinded, although we know of no biases that were introduced. By contrast, the lensing comparison was only conducted after the rest of the analysis was frozen and is thus fully blinded.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s9\" name=\"apjaad565s9\">9.\u00a0Summary</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We present our discovery and measurements of a lensed SN with a host-galaxy redshift of 2.22. The light curve of this SN most closely matches a SN\u00a0Ia, in both shape and color; there are only two known core-collapse templates within a factor of 20 of the best-fit SN\u00a0Ia likelihood (and with both of these requiring an additional two magnitudes of amplification to match the absolute magnitude of SNe Ia). We determine a robust host galaxy redshift of 2.2216\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0002, from a VLT X-shooter spectrum displaying multiple absorption features. Measurements of the spectrum, SED fitting of the spectrum and broadband photometry (from the rest-frame UV to <em>J</em> band), and measurement of the surface brightness profile consistently demonstrate a massive, low-SFR host galaxy. When combined with observational constraints on SN rates, the low-SFR environment implies that most of the SNe in this galaxy are expected to be SNe\u00a0Ia. When considering the likelihoods from the light curve fits to all SN subtypes, this leads to a 97.5% probability that this exceptional event is an SN\u00a0Ia. This makes it the highest redshift SN\u00a0Ia with a spectroscopic redshift and the highest redshift lensed SN of any type. Using the conventional SN\u00a0Ia standardization relation and assuming a Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>) cosmology, we estimate the SN amplification is <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn67.gif\" alt=\"${2.8}_{-0.5}^{+0.6}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{2.8}_{-0.5}^{+0.6}</script></span></span> in flux (1.1 mag), making this the most amplified SN\u00a0Ia discovered behind a galaxy cluster to date. We estimate only a 10% chance of finding a SN\u00a0Ia at or beyond this redshift (using the high-redshift SN rate model in Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib78\">2014</a>, up to <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03) at least this close to the center of one of our clusters (thus allowing it to be lensed), making this an exceptional discovery. The light-curve parameters of SN\u00a0SCP16C03 are close to the mode of the population distribution of lower redshift SNe, indicating that our first unbiased look at the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a02 SN\u00a0Ia population shows no evidence of strong population drift in these parameters. We also find consistency between the estimated amplification from the WL-derived map and our Hubble diagram residual. Based on our work, it appears that there were normal SNe\u00a0Ia ~11 Gyr before the present.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>, obtained at the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy Inc., under NASA contract NAS 5-26555. These observations are associated with programs GO-13677 and GO-14327, and the data can be obtained at <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"dataset\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T94017\" data-art-data-content-type=\"External repository\" data-toolbar-link=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T94017\" data-toolbar-title=\"MAST dataset\" data-art-data-type=\"ext-rep-mast\" id=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T94017\">10.17909/T94017</a>. Additionally based on observations collected at the European Organisation for Astronomical Research in the Southern Hemisphere under ESO program IDs 096.A-0926 and 296.A-5051. This publication makes use of data products from the Two Micron All Sky Survey, which is a joint project of the University of Massachusetts and the Infrared Processing and Analysis Center/California Institute of Technology, funded by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration and the National Science Foundation. Based on observations made with the Gran Telescopio Canarias (GTC), installed at the Spanish Observatorio del Roque de los Muchachos of the Instituto de Astrofsica de Canarias, on the island of La Palma. Additional support was provided from NASA ADAP NNH16AC25I. Support for programs GO-13677, GO-14327, and SNAP-14163 were provided by NASA through a grant from the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy Inc., under NASA contract NAS 5-26555.
