Finally, we acknowledge the anonymous referee, whose feedback greatly improved this manuscript.</p><p><em>Facilities:</em> <em>HST</em> (WFC3) - ,  VLT:Kueyen - ,  VLT:Yepun - ,  GTC. - \n</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565app1\" name=\"apjaad565app1\">Appendix A: Could Dust and Amplification Conspire to Enhance the Core-collapse Fraction?</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Without photometry that covers the rest-frame far-IR, it is difficult to entirely rule out obscured star formation from the host galaxy alone. If the star formation were obscured but undetected in our <tt>FAST</tt> fit, and the resulting core-collapse supernovae were still visible, our calculation of <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>) would overestimate the SN\u00a0Ia fraction and thereby increase the estimated SN\u00a0Ia probability. In this section, we address this scenario by evaluating the amount of star formation that could be hidden by dust, modifying Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>) to account for a hidden starburst, and then applying the See Change apparent magnitude detection efficiency curve to the modified rates for each SN type. We find that a hidden starburst as the source of this SN is very unlikely and does not alter its classification as a SN\u00a0Ia.</p><p>We modify Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>) to include mass and SFR estimates from the <tt>FAST</tt> SED fit and a separate starburst component. We also modify the core-collapse and Ia rates by the fraction of supernovae that are detectable by our survey, estimated on a grid of amplification and the starburst dust values, <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>. Both of these procedures are described below. Starting from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>), we parameterize the rates components as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaad565eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}\\begin{array}{rcl}{R}_{\\mathrm{CC}} & \\equiv & A\\,{\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\,{D}_{\\mathrm{CC}}\\\\ {R}_{\\mathrm{Ia}} & \\equiv & (B\\,{M}_{{\\rm{T}}}+C\\,{\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}){D}_{\\mathrm{Ia}},\\end{array}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with <em>A</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup>, <em>B</em>\u00a0=\u00a05.3\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u221214</sup>, <em>C</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.9\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22124</sup>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn68.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}})</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn69.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({M}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({M}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}})</script></span></span>, and <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> and <em>D</em><sub>Ia</sub> the fractions of the CC and Ia absolute magnitude distributions that are visible in our survey. As shown below, these fractions are estimated on a grid of amplification and <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> values. Since <em>M</em> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn70.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{M}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{M}</script></span></span> scale linearly with amplification, the detection fractions are the only components of this modified <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> equation that scale with amplification. The amount of starburst <em>M</em><sub>SB</sub> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn71.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}}}</script></span></span> allowed by the host galaxy photometry and spectroscopy, along with these detection fractions, are the critical components for investigating whether dusty star formation could bias our typing procedure toward high SN Ia rates.</p><p>To determine the amount of star formation that could be hidden in a dusty starburst component in the galaxy SED, we evaluate the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> value of the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> template with a starburst component added, on a grid of <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> and starburst flux values. For low starburst fluxes, this component primarily affects the GTC <em>g</em>-band, but at higher fluxes, it can affect the GTC <em>r</em> and <em>i</em> bands, the <em>HST</em> WFC3 UVIS <em>F</em>814<em>W</em>, and the bluest regions of the X-shooter spectrum. This provides a relatively tight constraint on the strength of a starburst component added to the SED (see, i.e., Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f5\">5</a>, where the GTC <em>r</em> and <em>i</em> and <em>HST F</em>814<em>W</em> are already slightly over-estimated by the <tt>FAST</tt> SED fit); at <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn72.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}\\,=0.07$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}\\,=0.07</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>, while at <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.0, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn73.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}=1.09$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}=1.09</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>. The \u0394<em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> improvement is only ~0.05 for the additional 2 degrees of freedom, meaning that the simpler, starburst-free model is preferred statistically. Still, in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>), we will include this best-fit <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn74.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}</script></span></span> in all SFR estimates for determination of <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> in the host galaxy.</p><p>To determine the detectable fractions of SNe of each type, we perform a simple Monte Carlo using the absolute magnitude distributions from Richardson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib73\">2014</a>), over a grid of <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> and amplification values. For each grid point, we draw absolute magnitudes from a normal distribution for each SN type given by Richardson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib73\">2014</a>). Using <tt>sncosmo</tt>, we build the spectral time-series for each type using SN\u00a02007md for Type IIp, SN\u00a02006fo for Type\u00a0Ibc,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn21\">39</a></sup>\n and the Hsiao template (Hsiao et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib37\">2007</a>) for Type\u00a0Ia. We apply the given <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> to the spectral time-series and calculate its apparent magnitude at maximum brightness in the <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> filter at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.2216, assuming a Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>) cosmology (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f8\">8</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Monte Carlo simulation of supernova detectability by type, as the amount of dust is increased, and accounting for the See Change detection efficiency versus magnitude. The ratio of the area under the filled histograms to the open histograms is <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> or <em>D</em><sub>Ia</sub> in Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>) (for <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub>, the IIp and Ibc SNe are combined with equal weight). We find that for reasonable amplification values, the observable CC SN rate is highly impacted by the truncation of the detection efficiency curve, leading the observable SN rate in this galaxy to move toward SNe Ia at higher starburst fractions (see Figure <a href=\"#apjaad565f9\">9</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>During the execution of the See Change survey, we planted blinded fake supernovae, and only unblinded after we had determined whether all objects were valuable enough to trigger <em>HST</em> follow-up. As a result, we have a robust measurement of our detection efficiency curve in apparent magnitude (see the black line in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f8\">8</a>). For each simulated supernova in our Monte Carlo simulation, we determine its likelihood of detection using this curve, so for each grid point in <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> and amplification, this provides<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn22\">40</a></sup>\n <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> and <em>D</em><sub>Ia</sub> for use in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>).</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f9\">9</a> shows the result for <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> after accounting for the starburst component and the detection efficiency of our survey. We find that for reasonable amplification values, the core-collapse rate is strongly truncated by the detection limit term in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>); the initial detection efficiency for CC SNe, <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> at <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00, does not cross 95% until an amplification of ~17. As a result, <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> increases with increasing dusty star formation, and when combined with the fact that even at low <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> the CC distributions are truncated (see, i.e., Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f8\">8</a>), this implies that our main typing algorithm underestimates the SN Ia probability by not including absolute magnitude.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0<em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> using the modified rates from Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>), as a function of <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>, for various amplification values with (solid) and without (dashed) the detection efficiency term. The <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> curves for different amplifications are shown only for <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.05. For reasonable values of amplification, the CC SN magnitude distribution is truncated by the detection efficiency, and <em>R</em><sub>CC</sub> is dominated by the quickly declining <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> term. For extremely high amplifications, the entire CC distribution is visible at low <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>, so <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01; in this scenario, a small enhancement to <em>R</em><sub>CC</sub> occurs as the hidden starburst component increases, causing <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> to briefly decline with <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> until <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> declines. We conclude that our primary typing algorithm, Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn1\">1</a>), using <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> as in Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>), is conservative for the SN Ia probability, since the observable <em>R</em><sub>CC</sub> is in reality lower due to our magnitude limits.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565app2\" name=\"apjaad565app2\">Appendix B: Is the Redshift 2.22 Falaxy the SN Host?</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>For use in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a>, we now estimate the probability that this SN is merely projected on the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy, rather than hosted by it. As in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a>, we consider the average likelihoods and rates for each type of SNe; for projected SNe, we must also consider these quantities over a range in redshift. We thus need three new ingredients: (1) The likelihood as a function of redshift for CC SNe and SNe\u00a0Ia. (2) The relative rates (per unit area, at the SN location) in the field and in the host. (3) The fraction of stellar mass and star formation (and thus rate) that is excluded for this SN, as we do not see a second galaxy on top of the presumptive host.</p><p>For the likelihood as a function of redshift, we run the photometric typing at a range of redshifts and compute the equivalent redshift width for each type. This is defined relative to the likelihood at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22, in the sense that averaging the likelihood over all redshifts gives the same answer as multiplying the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 value by the equivalent redshift width. We find this quantity is 0.35 for normal SNe\u00a0Ia, 0.23 for SN\u00a01991T-like SNe\u00a0Ia, 1.93 for SNe\u00a0Ib/c, and 0.93 for SNe\u00a0II. The CC SN values are larger than for SNe\u00a0Ia. The CC SNe have more heterogeneity, so they are consistent with the light curve over a wider range in redshift (i.e., a SN-only photo-<em>z</em> is not as accurate for a CC SN). As a special case, to test whether this SN could be an intra-cluster SN within MOO\u00a0J1014+0038 having a chance alignment with a background galaxy, we also run the typing at the cluster redshift of 1.23. The best-fit likelihood values are <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn75.gif\" alt=\"$\\gt 100$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\gt 100</script></span></span> times lower, strongly disfavoring this possibility.</p><p>Now we need to consider the relative rates compared to the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy. From Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib78\">2014</a>), the volumetric SN\u00a0Ia rate in this redshift range is around 0.5\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22124</sup> yr<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn76.gif\" alt=\"${h}_{70}^{3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{h}_{70}^{3}</script></span></span>. From Strolger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib96\">2015</a>), the volumetric CC SN rate in this redshift range is around 3.5\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22124</sup> yr<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn77.gif\" alt=\"${h}_{70}^{3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{h}_{70}^{3}</script></span></span>. We must now transform these rates (and the above rates in the host galaxy) to observer-frame-rate surface densities evaluated at the SN location. For convenience, we work in per square arcsec units (although this is an arbitrary choice). From the factors in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>), we estimate the global rates in the galaxy (0.0065 for SNe Ia and 0.0028 for CC SNe, both per rest-frame year); assuming the SN rates in the galaxy are roughly proportional to the rest-frame <em>B</em>-band (<em>F</em>140<em>W</em>) surface brightness (Heringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib36\">2017</a>), we can find the rate surface density. Using a small aperture at the location of the SN (on an <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> stacked image with no SN light), we measure the surface brightness. Multiplying this surface brightness by an area of one square arcsecond corresponds to 10% the luminosity of the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy. Thus, the SN rate per square arcsec is about 10% of the global galaxy rate, or 0.00028 for CC SNe and 0.00065 for SNe\u00a0Ia (both per rest-frame year). For the volumetric rates, we would naively find 0.00032 CC SNe and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn78.gif\" alt=\"$4.6\\times {10}^{-5}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">4.6\\times {10}^{-5}</script></span></span> SNe\u00a0Ia per square arcsec, per unit redshift, per rest-frame year, but we must lower these by the amplification (as the volume behind the cluster is lowered by the same factor). To be conservative, we use the (smaller) WL amplification estimate of 1.75, for rates of 0.00019 (CC SNe) and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn79.gif\" alt=\"$2.6\\times {10}^{-5}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">2.6\\times {10}^{-5}</script></span></span> (SNe Ia).</p><p>In some sense, the above volumetric rates count all expected galaxies along the line of sight, and thus double count the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy. They also count galaxies that would be clearly visible superposed on the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy (the vast majority of SN hosts). Thus, we must scale the rates by the fraction of star formation and stellar mass in galaxies too faint to detect in projection. Using only SN-free <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> images, we measure a 95% upper limit for a point-like galaxy<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn23\">41</a></sup>\n at the SN location of 0.2 <em>e</em><sup>\u2212</sup> s<sup>\u22121</sup> (after subtracting a smooth galaxy model for the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy). This is 28.2 (AB) as observed, or <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />28.8 after de-lensing. Assuming a redshift ~2.22, this corresponds to an absolute magnitude fainter than 16.2 in rest-frame <em>B</em>-band. Comparing to the rest-frame <em>B</em>-band luminosity function at this redshift (Marchesini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib58\">2007</a>), we see that our limit is almost six magnitudes fainter than <em>M</em>*. For a faint-end power law slope of \u22121.5, this would imply that 93% of <em>B</em>-band luminosity is excluded based on our upper limit. We apply this same factor to the SN rates; as we show, the probability of projection is small enough that reasonable variation in these assumptions does not affect the conclusion.</p><p>Finally, we complete the estimation of the projection probability, shown in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t6\">6</a>. We collect the relative likelihood values from Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a> and put them in the third column. The rates (per unit area and time for the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy, and per unit area, time, and \u0394<em>z</em> for projections) are summarized in the fourth column. These are the only two quantities needed in the case that the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy is the host. However, we multiply by the redshift equivalent width for the projections, giving more probability to types of SNe that are consistent with the light curve over a longer redshift baseline. In the fifth column, we take the product of these factors, giving the rate-normalized likelihood for each type of SNe. In the final column, we show these values after normalizing to one. Summing the probabilities in the last four rows, we find the probability of a projection is ~2\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaad565t6\" class=\"boxout class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://www.eso.org/sci/facilities/paranal/instruments/xshooter/doc/VLT-MAN-ESO-14650-4942_v87.pdf\">http://www.eso.org/sci/facilities/paranal/instruments/xshooter/doc/VLT-MAN-ESO-14650-4942_v87.pdf</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn5\" id=\"apjaad565fn5\">23\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The spline nodes were spaced less than 1 pixel (0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />128) apart near the core for the 1D radially varying spline, and 4 pixels apart for the 2D spline. Reasonable variations around these values returned virtually the same photometry.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn6\" id=\"apjaad565fn6\">24\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We use constant PSFs that do not follow <em>HST</em> focus changes. We allow for these changes by adding 0.02 mag (our measured dispersion on bright stars) in quadrature to the fit uncertainties.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn7\" id=\"apjaad565fn7\">25\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>All of the software tools referenced here are part of <tt>DrizzlePac</tt>: <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://drizzlepac.stsci.edu\">http://drizzlepac.stsci.edu</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn8\" id=\"apjaad565fn8\">26\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>As the fit patch is centered on the SN, any slope in the underlying galaxy light will cancel out. Thus, only the second derivative of the light can affect the photometry, and the second derivative at the SN location is much smaller than at the galaxy core.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn9\" id=\"apjaad565fn9\">27\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>SEP is a Python implementation of SExtractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts 1996): <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://sep.readthedocs.io\">https://sep.readthedocs.io</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn10\" id=\"apjaad565fn10\">28\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The major and minor radii are 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />19 and 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />83, respectively.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn11\" id=\"apjaad565fn11\">29\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>As a cross-check, we also perform an extraction centered at the SN location, using the same extraction profile weighting as derived for the galaxy extraction. This location is not fully independent, as it is 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7 offset from the core, and the seeing was about 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9. For this more local measurement, we marginalize the width of the emission component from 30 to 300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> (with a flat prior in this range) and the velocity offset (with a Gaussian prior of 300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>), as it may be localized in the galaxy. We again find no evidence of emission.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn12\" id=\"apjaad565fn12\">30\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The cosmology does not matter much here, but we use a flat \u039bCDM cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3089 (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>). Note that we also must account for possible additional extinction of the H<em>\u03b1</em> region compared to the older stars in the rest of the galaxy (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s5-2\">5.2)</a>. We thus use <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em></sub>\u00a0= <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn30.gif\" alt=\"$1.0\\pm 0.27$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">1.0\\pm 0.27</script></span></span>, rather than the <tt>FAST</tt> result of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn31.gif\" alt=\"$0.3\\pm 0.08$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.3\\pm 0.08</script></span></span>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn13\" id=\"apjaad565fn13\">31\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Marginalizing over the ellipticity also has the effect of making the fit less sensitive to lensing shear.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn14\" id=\"apjaad565fn14\">32\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565app1\">A</a>, we evaluate the feasibility of a starburst component added to the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> model, finding the scenario to be unlikely, and with only a small amount of obscured star formation allowed.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn15\" id=\"apjaad565fn15\">33\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://sncosmo.readthedocs.io\">http://sncosmo.readthedocs.io</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn16\" id=\"apjaad565fn16\">34\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Interestingly, despite its large uncertainty, the <em>K</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> measurement has sufficient constraining power to disfavor a single SN\u00a0Ib/c template (that would be otherwise compatible with the bluer <em>HST</em> data for an extinction in excess of 0.5 mag <em>E</em>(<em>B</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em>)); we show the remaining SN\u00a0Ib/c light-curve template that is not disfavored in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f7\">7</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn17\" id=\"apjaad565fn17\">35\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Here, we use the \"prompt-and-delayed\" model, in which the SN\u00a0Ia rate is modeled as a linear combination of stellar mass and star formation rate (Scannapieco &amp; Bildsten <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib85\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib85\">2005</a>; Mannucci et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib56\">2006</a>). We use coefficients from the Sullivan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib98\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib98\">2006</a>) analysis. We note that the Sullivan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib98\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib98\">2006</a>) coefficients are applied to the SFR averaged over the previous 500 Myr, rather than the instantaneous SFR. As the SFR was higher in the past for this galaxy, our procedure conservatively underestimates the SN Ia rate associated with star formation. We note that the SN Ia rate is dominated by the mass component for either SFR averaging time period, so our results are insensitive to this choice. In any case, this is a simple approximation to the true delay-time distribution, which is close to <em>t</em><sup>\u22121</sup> (see the review of Maoz &amp; Mannucci <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib57\">2012</a>). As an alternative, we also use the host-galaxy age and stellar mass with a <em>t</em><sup>\u22121</sup> delay-time distribution (normalized as in Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib78\">2014</a>), finding a somewhat higher SN\u00a0Ia rate. We use the prompt-and-delayed rate to be conservative.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn18\" id=\"apjaad565fn18\">36\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Assuming a Salpeter IMF (Salpeter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib83\">1955</a>) with all stars from 8 to 40 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> forming CC SNe, there will be 6.8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup> CC SNe per <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> of formed stars. This assumption is a reasonable match to observed CC rates and the cosmic star formation history (Madau &amp; Dickinson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib54\">2014</a>; Strolger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib96\">2015</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn19\" id=\"apjaad565fn19\">37\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The fractions here add up to 99% because they are rounded, but the final probability is precisely normalized in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn1\">1</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn20\" id=\"apjaad565fn20\">38\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In order to quantify this, we have to subtract the measurement uncertainties. When we do this assuming a split-normal distribution (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://github.com/rubind/salt2params\">https://github.com/rubind/salt2params</a>), we find that SN SCP16C03 is at the 64 \u00b1 20th percentile in <em>x</em><sub>1</sub>, and the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn65.gif\" alt=\"${67}_{-22}^{+16}\\mathrm{th}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{67}_{-22}^{+16}\\mathrm{th}</script></span></span> percentile in <em>c</em>. If the (mild) evidence for SN light in the epoch before the detection is real, the rise-time is ~20 days in rest-frame <em>B</em>-band. This is slower than average for a SN Ia, but still within the observed distribution (Hayden et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib35\">2010</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn21\" id=\"apjaad565fn21\">39\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>These two supernovae are the only core-collapse light curves with fit probabilities within a factor of 20 of the best Ia template in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s4\">4</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn22\" id=\"apjaad565fn22\">40\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In effect, this is a numerical integration of the multiplication of our detection efficiency curve with the apparent magnitude distribution after accounting for dust and the assumed Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>) cosmology.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn23\" id=\"apjaad565fn23\">41\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The FWHM of the PSF is 1 kpc at this redshift. Assuming a larger galaxy will give a somewhat weaker flux limit, but this has little quantitative effect on our conclusions. Assuming a magnitude limit one magnitude brighter only affects the projection probability by 0.1 percentage points.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">The Discovery of a Gravitationally Lensed Supernova Ia at Redshift 2.22</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">D. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F866%2F1%2F65\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/866/1/65</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>We present the discovery and measurements of a gravitationally lensed supernova (SN) behind the galaxy cluster MOO\u00a0J1014+0038. Based on multi-band <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> and Very Large Telescope (VLT) photometry of the supernova, and VLT spectroscopy of the host galaxy, we find a 97.5% probability that this SN is a SN\u00a0Ia, and a 2.5% chance of a CC SN. Our typing algorithm combines the shape and color of the light curve with the expected rates of each SN type in the host galaxy. With a redshift of 2.2216, this is the highest redshift SN\u00a0Ia discovered with a spectroscopic host-galaxy redshift. A further distinguishing feature is that the lensing cluster, at redshift 1.23, is the most distant to date to have an amplified SN. The SN lies in the middle of the color and light-curve shape distributions found at lower redshift, disfavoring strong evolution to <I>z</I>\u00a0=\u00a02.22. We estimate an amplification due to gravitational lensing of <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"${2.8}_{-0.5}^{+0.6}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/apjaad565ieqn1.gif\"></IMG> (1.10\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.23 mag)\u2014compatible with the value estimated from the weak-lensing-derived mass and the mass\u2013concentration relation from \u039bCDM simulations\u2014making it the most amplified SN\u00a0Ia discovered behind a galaxy cluster.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s1\" name=\"apjaad565s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Gravitational lensing by massive galaxy clusters offers an amplified and magnified view of the high-redshift universe. Several examples of supernovae (SNe) lensed by foreground clusters have been found in recent years (Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib32\">2009</a>; Amanullah et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib1\">2011</a>; Nordin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>; Patel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib68\">2014</a>; Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib42\">2015</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib75\">2015a</a>; Petrushevska et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib69\">2016</a>). Four of these cluster-lensed SNe have been of Type\u00a0Ia (SNe Ia), from which the amplification due to lensing has been determined. (Two additional SNe Ia that were lensed by field galaxies have also been found [Quimby et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib71\">2014</a>; Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib31\">2017</a>]; we summarize all referenced SNe in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t1\">1</a>.)</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaad565t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nOther High-redshift or Gravitationally Lensed SNe\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565t1\" (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib31\">2017</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">SCP16C03 (\"Joseph\")</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">2.22</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Spec</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Cluster</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">1.23</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">2.8</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Ia</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">This Work</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjaad565t1fna\" id=\"apjaad565t1fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>Multiple images; amplification is sum over all images.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/suppdata/apjaad565t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aad565\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/suppdata/apjaad565t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>The uniquely precise standardization possible with SNe\u00a0Ia provides amplification information, breaking the so-called mass-sheet degeneracy that is problematic for most shear-based lensing models, and permits independent direct tests of cluster mass models obtained from galaxy lensing (Nordin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>; Patel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib68\">2014</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib75\">2015a</a>). Lenses that produce multiple SN images provide additional model constraints (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib43\">2016b</a>). To date, the redshifts of gravitationally lensed background SNe\u00a0Ia have been at <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a01.39, a redshift range where even without lensing the complete (normal) SN\u00a0Ia population can be detected using single-orbit <em>HST</em> visits. In cases of stronger amplification of sources beyond the redshift reach of normal observations, tests of SN\u00a0Ia properties and rates over a larger look-back time become possible. Here we report the discovery of the most amplified SN\u00a0Ia behind a galaxy cluster ever found, with a spectroscopic host-galaxy redshift making it also the most distant.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s2\" name=\"apjaad565s2\">2.\u00a0Discovery</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The Supernova Cosmology Project's \"See\u00a0Change\" program (PI: Perlmutter) monitored 12 massive galaxy clusters in the redshift range 1.13\u20131.75 using the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3) with the goals of greatly expanding the range of the high-redshift SN\u00a0Ia Hubble diagram and accurately determining high-redshift cluster masses via weak lensing. We observed each cluster every 5 weeks for one orbit split between the UVIS <em>F</em>814<em>W</em>, the IR <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>, and the IR <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> filters. The supernova survey was very deep; our 50% completeness for simulated SN detection was AB 26.6 in <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>\u00a0+\u00a0<em>F</em>140<em>W</em> (B. Hayden et al. 2018, in preparation); this is ~1 mag fainter than a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.75 SN\u00a0Ia at maximum.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn1\">19</a></sup>\n When a promising Type\u00a0Ia supernova candidate was found, at least one extra visit was triggered and executed within 2\u20133 observer-frame weeks after the initial discovery. These extra visits provided better light-curve sampling, usually close to maximum light, and frequently extended the wavelength range with the IR <em>F</em>160<em>W</em> filter.</p><p>On 2016 February\u00a029\u00a0UTC, we searched images of the <em>WISE</em>-selected (Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib107\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib107\">2010</a>) massive galaxy cluster MOO\u00a0J1014+0038 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.23, B. Decker et al. 2018, in preparation; <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn5.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{200}=(5.6\\pm 0.6)\\times {10}^{14}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{200}=(5.6\\pm 0.6)\\times {10}^{14}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>, Brodwin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib11\">2015</a>), from the Massive and Distant Clusters of <em>WISE</em> Survey (MaDCoWS; Gettings et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib28\">2012</a>; Stanford et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib92\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib92\">2014</a>; Gonzalez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib30\">2015</a>). We detected a red (WFC3 <em>F</em>140<em>W</em>\u00a0=\u00a025.1, <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>F</em>140<em>W</em>\u00a0=1.4 AB mag; upper limit only in <em>F</em>814<em>W</em>) supernova at <em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a0153<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />52655, <em>\u03b4</em>\u00a0=\u00a0+0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />64041 (J2000, aligned to USNO-B1). This SN was internally designated as SN\u00a0SCP16C03,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn2\">20</a></sup>\n as it was the third SN found in this cluster field (alphabetically labeled cluster \"C\") in 2016. As shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f1\">1</a>, the SN lies on a red galaxy having a color of <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>F</em>140<em>W</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.5 AB mag and is located 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7 from the core of this galaxy (<em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a0153<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />52643, <em>\u03b4</em>\u00a0=\u00a0+0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />64025). We conclude that this galaxy must be the host galaxy; there are no other galaxies nearby, the light curve is incompatible with those possible from an intra-cluster SN within MOO\u00a0J1014+0038 (as discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a>), and the probability of a chance projection is negligible (~0.2%, as discussed in Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565app2\">B</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Color image of the central part of MOO\u00a0J1014+0038 made with an <em>F</em>814<em>W</em> stack and the <em>F</em>105<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160<em>W</em> data from our first triggered <em>HST</em> imaging near maximum light. The inset panel uses different scaling, and incorporates only IR data (<em>F</em>105<em>W</em>, <em>F</em>125<em>W</em>, and <em>F</em>160<em>W</em>). The compass arrows are 1'' in linear scale. Both panels use hyperbolic arcsine intensity scaling. In green, we show contours of our computed lensing amplification model (in magnitudes), described in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s8\">8</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s3\" name=\"apjaad565s3\">3.\u00a0Spectroscopic and Photometric Follow-up</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We activated ToO spectroscopic observations (PI: Hook) using X-shooter, the multi-wavelength medium resolution spectrograph on the Very Large Telescope (Vernet et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib104\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib104\">2011</a>). Five \"Observation Blocks\" were taken between 2016 March\u00a04\u20135 (UT), yielding a total integration time of 3.8, 4.4, and 5.0\u00a0hr in the X-shooter UVB, VIS, and NIR arms, respectively. Although the slit orientation captured both the host galaxy and the SN (shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f2\">2</a>), the galaxy was ~4 mag brighter, so only a host-galaxy spectrum could be extracted. The data were reduced to wavelength- and flux-calibrated, sky-subtracted, 2D spectra using the Reflex software (Freudling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib27\">2013</a>). Optimal 1D-extractions were determined using a combination of custom and <tt>IRAF</tt> (Tody <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib100\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib100\">1993</a>) routines.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn3\">21</a></sup>\n The extractions used a Gaussian profile in the cross-dispersion direction, the width of which was determined by fitting the trace in each (binned) 2D spectrum individually. These extractions were combined using the weighted mean to create a final 1D spectrum. We corrected for telluric absorption using a model atmosphere computed by the Line-by-Line Radiative Transfer Model (Clough et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib19\">1992</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib20\">2005</a>) retrieved through Telfit (Gullikson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib33\">2014</a>). Our observations were obtained at a low mean airmass of ~1.15 with a NIR slit width of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9 (matching the mean seeing). The X-shooter NIR arm is aligned with the optical at 13100 \u00c5<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn4\">22</a></sup>\n; the atmospheric differential refraction over the NIR wavelength range for this airmass range is &lt;0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1. For a Gaussian PSF (with FWHM 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9) shifted by 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1, the differential slit loss would be 2%. Thus, atmospheric differential refraction has a negligible impact on the calibration of our NIR spectrum. The host-galaxy spectrum is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f3\">3</a>. Balmer H<em>\u03b4</em> and H<em>\u03b2</em> absorption lines are clearly detected, as are the Ca\u00a0H&amp;K absorption lines. Mg\u00a0b is likely detected as well. These lines provide the main contribution to the weighted cross-correlation, as shown in the lower panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f3\">3</a>, yielding a redshift of 2.2216\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0002 for the host galaxy. No emission lines were detected (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s5-3\">5.3</a> for further details).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Illustration of the X-shooter slit orientation and location on the SN and host galaxy.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Top:\u00a0flux-calibrated, telluric corrected, de-lensed (by a factor of 2.8; see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s7\">7</a>), and binned (14 \u00c5 observer-frame) spectrum of the host galaxy (red line). Gray shaded regions indicate strong atmospheric absorption. The best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> model (fit to both the photometry and spectrum) is shown in black, convolved by the 305 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> velocity dispersion estimated by <tt>ppxf</tt>. Prominent features identified in both spectra are highlighted with light blue shading and labeled; the H<em>\u03b1</em> line is labeled for reference, but no prominent contribution to the cross-correlation is detected from it. Bottom:\u00a0the contribution to the weighted cross-correlation of each wavelength element of the spectrum. Regions of heavy atmospheric absorption are properly de-weighted, and the prominent features easily identified by eye provide a confident redshift determination of 2.2216\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0002.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>This SN was 15'' away from the center of a dense concentration of cluster galaxies. Our initial flux amplification estimate was ~2.2 , calculated by assuming that the optical and halo center were the same, and employing a Navarro\u2013Frenk\u2013White dark matter profile (Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib63\">1997</a>). Based on the likelihood that this would turn out to be a highly magnified, high-redshift SN\u00a0Ia, we applied for Director's Discretionary time on <em>HST</em> and were granted a three-orbit disruptive ToO to ensure a good measurement of the SN SED. We triggered <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>, <em>F</em>125<em>W</em>, and <em>F</em>160<em>W</em> imaging from our \"See Change\" allocation. We did not request <em>HST</em> grism spectroscopy, as the roll angle range available would not have allowed for a clean separation of the SN and its host. In addition to our triggered follow-up, we obtained additional WFC3 <em>F</em>814<em>W</em>, <em>F</em>105<em>W</em>, and <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> imaging at two more previously scheduled orbits (the cadenced search visits for discovering SNe). We also were granted Director's Discretionary time on VLT for imaging in the <em>K</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> band (PI: Nordin) with HAWK-I (Kissler-Patig et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib46\">2008</a>) in order to extend the wavelength range redder than is possible with <em>HST</em>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s4\" name=\"apjaad565s4\">4.\u00a0SN Photometry</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>With a pixel size of ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />128, the WFC3 IR channel undersamples the PSF, making it difficult to remove underlying galaxy light with image resampling and subtraction. In addition, this SN poses an unusual, but not unknown, challenge, as we have no reference (SN-free) images in <em>F</em>125<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160<em>W</em>, necessitating interpolation of the galaxy light over the SN position. To meet these needs, we have developed a \"forward modeling\" code described in Suzuki et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib99\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib99\">2012</a>) and Rubin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib81\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib81\">2013</a>) and updated for WFC3 IR in Nordin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>). This code fits each pixel as observed (flat-fielded \"flt\" images for WFC3 IR, CTE-corrected, flat-fielded \"flc\" images for WFC3 UVIS)\u2014that is, without resampling due to spatial alignment or correction for distortion. It does this by creating an analytic model of the scene, convolving that model with the PSF, and fitting it to the pixel values. In all cases, the effect on the flatfield of pixel area variations over the FoV are taken into account in order to properly account for spatial distortion.</p><p>For the host galaxy, our analytic model is a linear sum of an azimuthally symmetric spline (a 1D, radially varying component that is allowed to have ellipticity) and a 2D grid to capture azimuthal asymmetries.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn5\">23</a></sup>\n To these splines, we include a model for a point source representing the SN, with a different fitted amplitude in each epoch. As there are reference images in the cadenced IR filters (<em>F</em>105<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>140<em>W</em>), these can be used as a cross-check of our ability to do photometry in <em>F</em>125<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160<em>W</em>, for which references are lacking. We obtain nearly identical photometry in the <em>F</em>105<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> bands with and without modeling the reference images, although the uncertainties are smaller when including references. Testing with simulated SNe indicates no photometric biases at the 0.01 mag level, and accurate uncertainties,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn6\">24</a></sup>\n even without using reference images.</p><p>There is only weak evidence for SN light in the <em>F</em>814<em>W</em> filter, as expected for such a high redshift SN\u00a0Ia. The low S/N necessitates a different treatment than the IR, where the offset between the SN and host galaxy was treated as a fit parameter. To determine the location of the SN in the <em>F</em>814<em>W</em> images, we start by aligning the UVIS and IR exposures using <tt>TweakReg</tt>,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn7\">25</a></sup>\n and then drizzling to a common frame using <tt>AstroDrizzle</tt>. (These resampled images are not used for the SN photometry.) Then we perform PSF photometry on the UVIS images using the centroid transferred (using <tt>pixtopix</tt>) from that measured in the IR images, where the SN is detected. We fit for a spatially constant background underneath the SN, incorporating both the sky and underlying galaxy. Even the core of the galaxy is only detected at low S/N in each <em>F</em>814<em>W</em> exposure, so the second derivative<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn8\">26</a></sup>\n underneath the SN is small.</p><p>Bad weather allowed only one of the seven Hawk-I <em>K</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> observing blocks to be executed; 40 minutes of effective exposure time was obtained in excellent seeing (0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />27), enabling a measurement of the SN flux. We use the same host-galaxy model as for the WFC3 IR data, but with the offset of the SN from the host fixed, and employing field stars to derive the PSF. We determine the flux calibration (in AB mags) from the standard star FS19 and from the 2MASS (Skrutskie et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib91\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib91\">2006</a>) magnitudes of the field stars. Although the brightness uncertainty of 60% is too large to improve the distance measurements, the additional rest-frame <em>R</em>-band coverage helped with photometric classification.</p><p>We present the resulting SN photometry in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t2\">2</a>. The uncertainty in the host-galaxy subtraction in each band leads to correlations between the WFC3 IR photometry measurements (in the data for each filter), so we also provide the inverse covariance matrix in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t3\">3</a>. We take the WFC3 IR zeropoints from Nordin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib65\">2014</a>), converted to AB magnitudes, which take into account the WFC3 IR count-rate nonlinearity. For the UVIS F814W filter, we use the STScI-provided UVIS zeropoint, after correcting the encircled energy to an infinite aperture.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaad565t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 2.</b>\u00a0\nPhotometry for SN SCP16C03\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565t2\" photometric measurements. These data are correlated (within each band) due to uncertainty in the underlying host-galaxy light.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/suppdata/apjaad565t3_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aad565\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/suppdata/apjaad565t3_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565s5\" name=\"apjaad565s5\">5.\u00a0Host-galaxy Properties</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this section, we present our measurements of the host-galaxy properties from spectroscopy and imaging, and summarize the evidence that the galaxy is old for its redshift and therefore lacking significant star formation in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s5-5\">5.5</a>.</p><h3 id=\"apjaad565s5-1\" name=\"apjaad565s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Host-galaxy Photometry</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We perform photometry of the host galaxy on aligned, stacked (SN-free) images using <tt>SEP.</tt><sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn9\">27</a></sup>\n To minimize contamination by light from the nearby galaxies, we use an elliptical aperture set to 1.2 times the Kron radius (Kron <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib49\">1980</a>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn10\">28</a></sup>\n In addition to the <em>HST</em> WFC3 photometry, we use archival <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC CH1 and CH2 data (A. H. Gonzalez et al. 2018, in preparation), and we obtained bluer <em>g</em>', <em>r</em>', and <em>i</em>' photometry of the host with the OSIRIS instrument at the Gran Telescopio Canarias (GTC). We determine the zeropoints of this photometry using an SDSS star in the GTC field of view (both the star and galaxy are measured with the same elliptical aperture). We present the host-galaxy photometry in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t4\">4</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaad565t4\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 4.</b>\u00a0\nHost-galaxy Photometry\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565t4\"
