The light curve of this SN most closely matches a SN\u00a0Ia, in both shape and color; there are only two known core-collapse templates within a factor of 20 of the best-fit SN\u00a0Ia likelihood (and with both of these requiring an additional two magnitudes of amplification to match the absolute magnitude of SNe Ia). We determine a robust host galaxy redshift of 2.2216\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0002, from a VLT X-shooter spectrum displaying multiple absorption features. Measurements of the spectrum, SED fitting of the spectrum and broadband photometry (from the rest-frame UV to <em>J</em> band), and measurement of the surface brightness profile consistently demonstrate a massive, low-SFR host galaxy. When combined with observational constraints on SN rates, the low-SFR environment implies that most of the SNe in this galaxy are expected to be SNe\u00a0Ia. When considering the likelihoods from the light curve fits to all SN subtypes, this leads to a 97.5% probability that this exceptional event is an SN\u00a0Ia. This makes it the highest redshift SN\u00a0Ia with a spectroscopic redshift and the highest redshift lensed SN of any type. Using the conventional SN\u00a0Ia standardization relation and assuming a Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>) cosmology, we estimate the SN amplification is <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn67.gif\" alt=\"${2.8}_{-0.5}^{+0.6}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{2.8}_{-0.5}^{+0.6}</script></span></span> in flux (1.1 mag), making this the most amplified SN\u00a0Ia discovered behind a galaxy cluster to date. We estimate only a 10% chance of finding a SN\u00a0Ia at or beyond this redshift (using the high-redshift SN rate model in Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib78\">2014</a>, up to <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03) at least this close to the center of one of our clusters (thus allowing it to be lensed), making this an exceptional discovery. The light-curve parameters of SN\u00a0SCP16C03 are close to the mode of the population distribution of lower redshift SNe, indicating that our first unbiased look at the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a02 SN\u00a0Ia population shows no evidence of strong population drift in these parameters. We also find consistency between the estimated amplification from the WL-derived map and our Hubble diagram residual. Based on our work, it appears that there were normal SNe\u00a0Ia ~11 Gyr before the present.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>, obtained at the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy Inc., under NASA contract NAS 5-26555. These observations are associated with programs GO-13677 and GO-14327, and the data can be obtained at <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"dataset\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T94017\" data-art-data-content-type=\"External repository\" data-toolbar-link=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T94017\" data-toolbar-title=\"MAST dataset\" data-art-data-type=\"ext-rep-mast\" id=\"https://doi.org/10.17909/T94017\">10.17909/T94017</a>. Additionally based on observations collected at the European Organisation for Astronomical Research in the Southern Hemisphere under ESO program IDs 096.A-0926 and 296.A-5051. This publication makes use of data products from the Two Micron All Sky Survey, which is a joint project of the University of Massachusetts and the Infrared Processing and Analysis Center/California Institute of Technology, funded by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration and the National Science Foundation. Based on observations made with the Gran Telescopio Canarias (GTC), installed at the Spanish Observatorio del Roque de los Muchachos of the Instituto de Astrofsica de Canarias, on the island of La Palma. Additional support was provided from NASA ADAP NNH16AC25I. Support for programs GO-13677, GO-14327, and SNAP-14163 were provided by NASA through a grant from the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy Inc., under NASA contract NAS 5-26555. Additional support was provided by the Director, Office of Science, Office of High Energy Physics, of the U.S. Department of Energy under contract No. DE-AC02-05CH11231. M.J. Jee acknowledges support for the current research from the National Research Foundation of Korea under the programs 2017R1A2B2004644 and 2017R1A4A1015178. The work of P.E. and D.S. was carried out at Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology, under a contract with NASA. We thank Rachel Bezanson for useful feedback on our assessment of the host-galaxy properties. We also thank Dan Maoz for a discussion of the WFC3 pipeline units. Finally, we acknowledge the anonymous referee, whose feedback greatly improved this manuscript.</p><p><em>Facilities:</em> <em>HST</em> (WFC3) - ,  VLT:Kueyen - ,  VLT:Yepun - ,  GTC. - \n</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565app1\" name=\"apjaad565app1\">Appendix A: Could Dust and Amplification Conspire to Enhance the Core-collapse Fraction?</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Without photometry that covers the rest-frame far-IR, it is difficult to entirely rule out obscured star formation from the host galaxy alone. If the star formation were obscured but undetected in our <tt>FAST</tt> fit, and the resulting core-collapse supernovae were still visible, our calculation of <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>) would overestimate the SN\u00a0Ia fraction and thereby increase the estimated SN\u00a0Ia probability. In this section, we address this scenario by evaluating the amount of star formation that could be hidden by dust, modifying Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>) to account for a hidden starburst, and then applying the See Change apparent magnitude detection efficiency curve to the modified rates for each SN type. We find that a hidden starburst as the source of this SN is very unlikely and does not alter its classification as a SN\u00a0Ia.</p><p>We modify Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>) to include mass and SFR estimates from the <tt>FAST</tt> SED fit and a separate starburst component. We also modify the core-collapse and Ia rates by the fraction of supernovae that are detectable by our survey, estimated on a grid of amplification and the starburst dust values, <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>. Both of these procedures are described below. Starting from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>), we parameterize the rates components as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaad565eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}\\begin{array}{rcl}{R}_{\\mathrm{CC}} & \\equiv & A\\,{\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\,{D}_{\\mathrm{CC}}\\\\ {R}_{\\mathrm{Ia}} & \\equiv & (B\\,{M}_{{\\rm{T}}}+C\\,{\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}){D}_{\\mathrm{Ia}},\\end{array}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with <em>A</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup>, <em>B</em>\u00a0=\u00a05.3\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u221214</sup>, <em>C</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.9\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22124</sup>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn68.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}})</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn69.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({M}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{{\\rm{T}}}\\equiv ({M}_{\\mathrm{FAST}}+{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}})</script></span></span>, and <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> and <em>D</em><sub>Ia</sub> the fractions of the CC and Ia absolute magnitude distributions that are visible in our survey. As shown below, these fractions are estimated on a grid of amplification and <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> values. Since <em>M</em> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn70.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{M}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{M}</script></span></span> scale linearly with amplification, the detection fractions are the only components of this modified <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> equation that scale with amplification. The amount of starburst <em>M</em><sub>SB</sub> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn71.gif\" alt=\"$\\dot{{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\dot{{M}_{\\mathrm{SB}}}</script></span></span> allowed by the host galaxy photometry and spectroscopy, along with these detection fractions, are the critical components for investigating whether dusty star formation could bias our typing procedure toward high SN Ia rates.</p><p>To determine the amount of star formation that could be hidden in a dusty starburst component in the galaxy SED, we evaluate the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> value of the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> template with a starburst component added, on a grid of <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> and starburst flux values. For low starburst fluxes, this component primarily affects the GTC <em>g</em>-band, but at higher fluxes, it can affect the GTC <em>r</em> and <em>i</em> bands, the <em>HST</em> WFC3 UVIS <em>F</em>814<em>W</em>, and the bluest regions of the X-shooter spectrum. This provides a relatively tight constraint on the strength of a starburst component added to the SED (see, i.e., Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f5\">5</a>, where the GTC <em>r</em> and <em>i</em> and <em>HST F</em>814<em>W</em> are already slightly over-estimated by the <tt>FAST</tt> SED fit); at <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn72.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}\\,=0.07$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}\\,=0.07</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>, while at <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.0, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn73.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}=1.09$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}=1.09</script></span></span> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>. The \u0394<em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> improvement is only ~0.05 for the additional 2 degrees of freedom, meaning that the simpler, starburst-free model is preferred statistically. Still, in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>), we will include this best-fit <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn74.gif\" alt=\"${\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\dot{M}}_{\\mathrm{SB}}</script></span></span> in all SFR estimates for determination of <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> in the host galaxy.</p><p>To determine the detectable fractions of SNe of each type, we perform a simple Monte Carlo using the absolute magnitude distributions from Richardson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib73\">2014</a>), over a grid of <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> and amplification values. For each grid point, we draw absolute magnitudes from a normal distribution for each SN type given by Richardson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib73\">2014</a>). Using <tt>sncosmo</tt>, we build the spectral time-series for each type using SN\u00a02007md for Type IIp, SN\u00a02006fo for Type\u00a0Ibc,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn21\">39</a></sup>\n and the Hsiao template (Hsiao et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib37\">2007</a>) for Type\u00a0Ia. We apply the given <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> to the spectral time-series and calculate its apparent magnitude at maximum brightness in the <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> filter at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.2216, assuming a Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>) cosmology (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f8\">8</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Monte Carlo simulation of supernova detectability by type, as the amount of dust is increased, and accounting for the See Change detection efficiency versus magnitude. The ratio of the area under the filled histograms to the open histograms is <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> or <em>D</em><sub>Ia</sub> in Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>) (for <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub>, the IIp and Ibc SNe are combined with equal weight). We find that for reasonable amplification values, the observable CC SN rate is highly impacted by the truncation of the detection efficiency curve, leading the observable SN rate in this galaxy to move toward SNe Ia at higher starburst fractions (see Figure <a href=\"#apjaad565f9\">9</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>During the execution of the See Change survey, we planted blinded fake supernovae, and only unblinded after we had determined whether all objects were valuable enough to trigger <em>HST</em> follow-up. As a result, we have a robust measurement of our detection efficiency curve in apparent magnitude (see the black line in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f8\">8</a>). For each simulated supernova in our Monte Carlo simulation, we determine its likelihood of detection using this curve, so for each grid point in <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> and amplification, this provides<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn22\">40</a></sup>\n <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> and <em>D</em><sub>Ia</sub> for use in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>).</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f9\">9</a> shows the result for <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> after accounting for the starburst component and the detection efficiency of our survey. We find that for reasonable amplification values, the core-collapse rate is strongly truncated by the detection limit term in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>); the initial detection efficiency for CC SNe, <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> at <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00, does not cross 95% until an amplification of ~17. As a result, <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> increases with increasing dusty star formation, and when combined with the fact that even at low <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> the CC distributions are truncated (see, i.e., Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f8\">8</a>), this implies that our main typing algorithm underestimates the SN Ia probability by not including absolute magnitude.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaad565f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaad565f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0<em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> using the modified rates from Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn4\">4</a>), as a function of <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>, for various amplification values with (solid) and without (dashed) the detection efficiency term. The <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> curves for different amplifications are shown only for <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.05. For reasonable values of amplification, the CC SN magnitude distribution is truncated by the detection efficiency, and <em>R</em><sub>CC</sub> is dominated by the quickly declining <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> term. For extremely high amplifications, the entire CC distribution is visible at low <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub>, so <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01; in this scenario, a small enhancement to <em>R</em><sub>CC</sub> occurs as the hidden starburst component increases, causing <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> to briefly decline with <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>,SB</sub> until <em>D</em><sub>CC</sub> declines. We conclude that our primary typing algorithm, Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn1\">1</a>), using <em>f</em><sub>Ia</sub> as in Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>), is conservative for the SN Ia probability, since the observable <em>R</em><sub>CC</sub> is in reality lower due to our magnitude limits.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaad565f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadFigure/figure/apjaad565f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaad565f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/866/1/65/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaad565f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaad565app2\" name=\"apjaad565app2\">Appendix B: Is the Redshift 2.22 Falaxy the SN Host?</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>For use in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a>, we now estimate the probability that this SN is merely projected on the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy, rather than hosted by it. As in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a>, we consider the average likelihoods and rates for each type of SNe; for projected SNe, we must also consider these quantities over a range in redshift. We thus need three new ingredients: (1) The likelihood as a function of redshift for CC SNe and SNe\u00a0Ia. (2) The relative rates (per unit area, at the SN location) in the field and in the host. (3) The fraction of stellar mass and star formation (and thus rate) that is excluded for this SN, as we do not see a second galaxy on top of the presumptive host.</p><p>For the likelihood as a function of redshift, we run the photometric typing at a range of redshifts and compute the equivalent redshift width for each type. This is defined relative to the likelihood at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22, in the sense that averaging the likelihood over all redshifts gives the same answer as multiplying the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 value by the equivalent redshift width. We find this quantity is 0.35 for normal SNe\u00a0Ia, 0.23 for SN\u00a01991T-like SNe\u00a0Ia, 1.93 for SNe\u00a0Ib/c, and 0.93 for SNe\u00a0II. The CC SN values are larger than for SNe\u00a0Ia. The CC SNe have more heterogeneity, so they are consistent with the light curve over a wider range in redshift (i.e., a SN-only photo-<em>z</em> is not as accurate for a CC SN). As a special case, to test whether this SN could be an intra-cluster SN within MOO\u00a0J1014+0038 having a chance alignment with a background galaxy, we also run the typing at the cluster redshift of 1.23. The best-fit likelihood values are <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn75.gif\" alt=\"$\\gt 100$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\gt 100</script></span></span> times lower, strongly disfavoring this possibility.</p><p>Now we need to consider the relative rates compared to the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy. From Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib78\">2014</a>), the volumetric SN\u00a0Ia rate in this redshift range is around 0.5\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22124</sup> yr<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn76.gif\" alt=\"${h}_{70}^{3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{h}_{70}^{3}</script></span></span>. From Strolger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib96\">2015</a>), the volumetric CC SN rate in this redshift range is around 3.5\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22124</sup> yr<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22123</sup> <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn77.gif\" alt=\"${h}_{70}^{3}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{h}_{70}^{3}</script></span></span>. We must now transform these rates (and the above rates in the host galaxy) to observer-frame-rate surface densities evaluated at the SN location. For convenience, we work in per square arcsec units (although this is an arbitrary choice). From the factors in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn2\">2</a>), we estimate the global rates in the galaxy (0.0065 for SNe Ia and 0.0028 for CC SNe, both per rest-frame year); assuming the SN rates in the galaxy are roughly proportional to the rest-frame <em>B</em>-band (<em>F</em>140<em>W</em>) surface brightness (Heringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib36\">2017</a>), we can find the rate surface density. Using a small aperture at the location of the SN (on an <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> stacked image with no SN light), we measure the surface brightness. Multiplying this surface brightness by an area of one square arcsecond corresponds to 10% the luminosity of the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy. Thus, the SN rate per square arcsec is about 10% of the global galaxy rate, or 0.00028 for CC SNe and 0.00065 for SNe\u00a0Ia (both per rest-frame year). For the volumetric rates, we would naively find 0.00032 CC SNe and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn78.gif\" alt=\"$4.6\\times {10}^{-5}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">4.6\\times {10}^{-5}</script></span></span> SNe\u00a0Ia per square arcsec, per unit redshift, per rest-frame year, but we must lower these by the amplification (as the volume behind the cluster is lowered by the same factor). To be conservative, we use the (smaller) WL amplification estimate of 1.75, for rates of 0.00019 (CC SNe) and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn79.gif\" alt=\"$2.6\\times {10}^{-5}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">2.6\\times {10}^{-5}</script></span></span> (SNe Ia).</p><p>In some sense, the above volumetric rates count all expected galaxies along the line of sight, and thus double count the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy. They also count galaxies that would be clearly visible superposed on the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy (the vast majority of SN hosts). Thus, we must scale the rates by the fraction of star formation and stellar mass in galaxies too faint to detect in projection. Using only SN-free <em>F</em>140<em>W</em> images, we measure a 95% upper limit for a point-like galaxy<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaad565fn23\">41</a></sup>\n at the SN location of 0.2 <em>e</em><sup>\u2212</sup> s<sup>\u22121</sup> (after subtracting a smooth galaxy model for the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy). This is 28.2 (AB) as observed, or <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />28.8 after de-lensing. Assuming a redshift ~2.22, this corresponds to an absolute magnitude fainter than 16.2 in rest-frame <em>B</em>-band. Comparing to the rest-frame <em>B</em>-band luminosity function at this redshift (Marchesini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib58\">2007</a>), we see that our limit is almost six magnitudes fainter than <em>M</em>*. For a faint-end power law slope of \u22121.5, this would imply that 93% of <em>B</em>-band luminosity is excluded based on our upper limit. We apply this same factor to the SN rates; as we show, the probability of projection is small enough that reasonable variation in these assumptions does not affect the conclusion.</p><p>Finally, we complete the estimation of the projection probability, shown in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaad565t6\">6</a>. We collect the relative likelihood values from Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s6\">6</a> and put them in the third column. The rates (per unit area and time for the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy, and per unit area, time, and \u0394<em>z</em> for projections) are summarized in the fourth column. These are the only two quantities needed in the case that the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.22 galaxy is the host. However, we multiply by the redshift equivalent width for the projections, giving more probability to types of SNe that are consistent with the light curve over a longer redshift baseline. In the fifth column, we take the product of these factors, giving the rate-normalized likelihood for each type of SNe. In the final column, we show these values after normalizing to one. Summing the probabilities in the last four rows, we find the probability of a projection is ~2\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaad565t6\" class=\"boxout name=\"apjaad565fn1\" id=\"apjaad565fn1\">19\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>When stacking all the epochs together, we reach 5<em>\u03c3</em> point-source depths of 28.0 AB mag in <em>F</em>105<em>W</em> and 27.9 AB mag in <em>F</em>140<em>W</em>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn2\" id=\"apjaad565fn2\">20\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Nicknamed \"Joseph.\"</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn3\" id=\"apjaad565fn3\">21\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>IRAF is distributed by the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy Inc., under cooperative agreement with the National Science Foundation.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn4\" id=\"apjaad565fn4\">22\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>X-shooter user manual, Section 2.2.1.7: <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://www.eso.org/sci/facilities/paranal/instruments/xshooter/doc/VLT-MAN-ESO-14650-4942_v87.pdf\">http://www.eso.org/sci/facilities/paranal/instruments/xshooter/doc/VLT-MAN-ESO-14650-4942_v87.pdf</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn5\" id=\"apjaad565fn5\">23\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The spline nodes were spaced less than 1 pixel (0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />128) apart near the core for the 1D radially varying spline, and 4 pixels apart for the 2D spline. Reasonable variations around these values returned virtually the same photometry.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn6\" id=\"apjaad565fn6\">24\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We use constant PSFs that do not follow <em>HST</em> focus changes. We allow for these changes by adding 0.02 mag (our measured dispersion on bright stars) in quadrature to the fit uncertainties.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn7\" id=\"apjaad565fn7\">25\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>All of the software tools referenced here are part of <tt>DrizzlePac</tt>: <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://drizzlepac.stsci.edu\">http://drizzlepac.stsci.edu</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn8\" id=\"apjaad565fn8\">26\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>As the fit patch is centered on the SN, any slope in the underlying galaxy light will cancel out. Thus, only the second derivative of the light can affect the photometry, and the second derivative at the SN location is much smaller than at the galaxy core.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn9\" id=\"apjaad565fn9\">27\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>SEP is a Python implementation of SExtractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts 1996): <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://sep.readthedocs.io\">https://sep.readthedocs.io</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn10\" id=\"apjaad565fn10\">28\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The major and minor radii are 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />19 and 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />83, respectively.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn11\" id=\"apjaad565fn11\">29\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>As a cross-check, we also perform an extraction centered at the SN location, using the same extraction profile weighting as derived for the galaxy extraction. This location is not fully independent, as it is 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7 offset from the core, and the seeing was about 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9. For this more local measurement, we marginalize the width of the emission component from 30 to 300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> (with a flat prior in this range) and the velocity offset (with a Gaussian prior of 300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>), as it may be localized in the galaxy. We again find no evidence of emission.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn12\" id=\"apjaad565fn12\">30\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The cosmology does not matter much here, but we use a flat \u039bCDM cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3089 (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>). Note that we also must account for possible additional extinction of the H<em>\u03b1</em> region compared to the older stars in the rest of the galaxy (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s5-2\">5.2)</a>. We thus use <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em></sub>\u00a0= <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn30.gif\" alt=\"$1.0\\pm 0.27$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">1.0\\pm 0.27</script></span></span>, rather than the <tt>FAST</tt> result of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn31.gif\" alt=\"$0.3\\pm 0.08$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.3\\pm 0.08</script></span></span>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn13\" id=\"apjaad565fn13\">31\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Marginalizing over the ellipticity also has the effect of making the fit less sensitive to lensing shear.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn14\" id=\"apjaad565fn14\">32\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565app1\">A</a>, we evaluate the feasibility of a starburst component added to the best-fit <tt>FAST</tt> model, finding the scenario to be unlikely, and with only a small amount of obscured star formation allowed.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn15\" id=\"apjaad565fn15\">33\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://sncosmo.readthedocs.io\">http://sncosmo.readthedocs.io</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn16\" id=\"apjaad565fn16\">34\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Interestingly, despite its large uncertainty, the <em>K</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> measurement has sufficient constraining power to disfavor a single SN\u00a0Ib/c template (that would be otherwise compatible with the bluer <em>HST</em> data for an extinction in excess of 0.5 mag <em>E</em>(<em>B</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em>)); we show the remaining SN\u00a0Ib/c light-curve template that is not disfavored in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaad565f7\">7</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn17\" id=\"apjaad565fn17\">35\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Here, we use the \"prompt-and-delayed\" model, in which the SN\u00a0Ia rate is modeled as a linear combination of stellar mass and star formation rate (Scannapieco &amp; Bildsten <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib85\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib85\">2005</a>; Mannucci et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib56\">2006</a>). We use coefficients from the Sullivan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib98\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib98\">2006</a>) analysis. We note that the Sullivan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib98\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib98\">2006</a>) coefficients are applied to the SFR averaged over the previous 500 Myr, rather than the instantaneous SFR. As the SFR was higher in the past for this galaxy, our procedure conservatively underestimates the SN Ia rate associated with star formation. We note that the SN Ia rate is dominated by the mass component for either SFR averaging time period, so our results are insensitive to this choice. In any case, this is a simple approximation to the true delay-time distribution, which is close to <em>t</em><sup>\u22121</sup> (see the review of Maoz &amp; Mannucci <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib57\">2012</a>). As an alternative, we also use the host-galaxy age and stellar mass with a <em>t</em><sup>\u22121</sup> delay-time distribution (normalized as in Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib78\">2014</a>), finding a somewhat higher SN\u00a0Ia rate. We use the prompt-and-delayed rate to be conservative.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn18\" id=\"apjaad565fn18\">36\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Assuming a Salpeter IMF (Salpeter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib83\">1955</a>) with all stars from 8 to 40 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> forming CC SNe, there will be 6.8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u22123</sup> CC SNe per <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> of formed stars. This assumption is a reasonable match to observed CC rates and the cosmic star formation history (Madau &amp; Dickinson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib54\">2014</a>; Strolger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib96\">2015</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn19\" id=\"apjaad565fn19\">37\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The fractions here add up to 99% because they are rounded, but the final probability is precisely normalized in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaad565eqn1\">1</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn20\" id=\"apjaad565fn20\">38\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In order to quantify this, we have to subtract the measurement uncertainties. When we do this assuming a split-normal distribution (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"https://github.com/rubind/salt2params\">https://github.com/rubind/salt2params</a>), we find that SN SCP16C03 is at the 64 \u00b1 20th percentile in <em>x</em><sub>1</sub>, and the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/866/1/65/Full/apjaad565ieqn65.gif\" alt=\"${67}_{-22}^{+16}\\mathrm{th}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{67}_{-22}^{+16}\\mathrm{th}</script></span></span> percentile in <em>c</em>. If the (mild) evidence for SN light in the epoch before the detection is real, the rise-time is ~20 days in rest-frame <em>B</em>-band. This is slower than average for a SN Ia, but still within the observed distribution (Hayden et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib35\">2010</a>).</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn21\" id=\"apjaad565fn21\">39\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>These two supernovae are the only core-collapse light curves with fit probabilities within a factor of 20 of the best Ia template in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaad565s4\">4</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn22\" id=\"apjaad565fn22\">40\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>In effect, this is a numerical integration of the multiplication of our detection efficiency curve with the apparent magnitude distribution after accounting for dust and the assumed Planck Collaboration et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaad565bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaad565bib70\">2016</a>) cosmology.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaad565fn23\" id=\"apjaad565fn23\">41\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The FWHM of the PSF is 1 kpc at this redshift. Assuming a larger galaxy will give a somewhat weaker flux limit, but this has little quantitative effect on our conclusions. Assuming a magnitude limit one magnitude brighter only affects the projection probability by 0.1 percentage points.</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start Based on multi-band Hubble Space Telescope and Very Large Telescope (VLT) photometry of the supernova, and VLT spectroscopy of the host galaxy, we find a 97.5% probability that this SN is a SN Ia, and a 2.5% chance of a CC SN. Our typing algorithm combines the shape and color of the light curve with the expected rates of each SN type in the host galaxy. With a redshift of 2.2216, this is the highest redshift SN Ia discovered with a spectroscopic host-galaxy redshift. A further distinguishing feature is that the lensing cluster, at redshift 1.23, is the most distant to date to have an amplified SN. The SN lies in the middle of the color and light-curve shape distributions found at lower redshift, disfavoring strong evolution to z = 2.22. We estimate an amplification due to gravitational lensing of {2.8}<SUB>-0.5</SUB><SUP>+0.6</SUP> (1.10 \u00b1 0.23 mag)\u2014compatible with the value estimated from the weak-lensing-derived mass and the mass-concentration relation from \u039bCDM simulations\u2014making it the most amplified SN Ia discovered behind a galaxy cluster.", "start_page": "65", "authors": ["Rubin, D.", "Hayden, B.", "Huang, X.", "Aldering, G.", "Amanullah, R.", "Barbary, K.", "Boone, K.", "Brodwin, M.", "Deustua, S.
